<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_120216436">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/120216436" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin" class="left imgcol" title="Mike (the Paladin)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450193915p2/2187043.jpg" alt="Mike (the Paladin)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/120216436?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 15, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin" class="user" title="Mike (the Paladin)">Mike (the Paladin)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/87809.The_Picture_of_Dorian_Gray" title="Paperback 9780140431872" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2187043-mike-the-paladin?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2187043-mike-the-paladin?shelf=classic-horror" class="actionLinkLite">classic-horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer120216436">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17112594022742804996">I have been meaning to read this book for...maybe 40 or 50 years, closer to 40 I suppose. It's one of those classics that you always mean to get to. I just never had.<br /><br />Like many people (I suppose) my knowledge of Oscar Wilde is fairly sketchy and mostly surface. It's the kind of thing you get from quotes and literary sketches. This book made me a little more curious about the famous rebel.<br /><br />Most people, even those who haven't read the novel will be aware of the background story here. Dorian Gray in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17112594022742804996">I have been meaning to read this book for...maybe 40 or 50 years, closer to 40 I suppose. It's one of those classics that you always mean to get to. I just never had.<br /><br />Like many people (I suppose) my knowledge of Oscar Wilde is fairly sketchy and mostly surface. It's the kind of thing you get from quotes and literary sketches. This book made me a little more curious about the famous rebel.<br /><br />Most people, even those who haven't read the novel will be aware of the background story here. Dorian Gray in the "glory" of youth and being an exceptionally attractive young man anyway looks on his own visage in a portrait painted by his "friend" Basil Hallward. Having been influenced (it is supposed) by his new acquaintance Lord Henry Wotton, Dorian dreads the loss of such youth and beauty and "wishes" the portrait could suffer the ravages of time and life while sparing him such...he says he'd go so far as to give his soul for this. <br /><br />This book is a product of it's time, maybe a little more florid in some ways than we'd find now. Wilde takes his time introducing us to the characters and laying the ground work before he introduces the fantastic and horrific elements of the story which creep up on us a bit at a time. Sir Henry is usually taken as the background villain of the piece, Mephistopheles to Dorian's Faust... but to an extent I think that's a bit misleading and I don't think Wilde saw it that way.<br /><br />This is an excellent work and I think most will find it enjoyable. It is not only a well written and deeply characterized book...it carries an understated and pervasive type of horror that might just require the reader to think.<br /><br />5 stars.<br /><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[The description of Dorian as "innocent" and "good" when he's young and when his portrait is painted is to an extent, only the description Dorian accepts about himself. Even when we first meet Dorian he is quite willing to manipulate Basil. <br /><br />Henry who is thought of as the Villain of the piece, is basically a fool. He knows not nearly so much as he thinks he does in spite of his self assurance. I think that Wilde showed us the flaws in Henry's ideas...just as he showed us the shallowness of Dorian. Dorian is constantly making vows to reform, decisions "to be better". Wilde "probably" didn't think much of this sort of thing as he pokes fun at it throughout his life. Dorian is a bit more serious in his failures as he graduates from manipulation, to destruction of life, to murder and so on. It's interesting to follow Dorian as he bemoans how these tragedies "effect him".<br /><br />So..my take... Lord Henry is a fool, Dorian is shallow and evil.<br /><br />By the way, I love the sort of "abrupt" ending with no cometary at all...just the final scene.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17112594022742804996">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_938879128">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/938879128" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="left imgcol" title="Apatt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" alt="Apatt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/938879128?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="user" title="Apatt">Apatt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer938879128">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14971157547436964364"><blockquote> <em>“He was always late on principle, his principle being that punctuality is the thief of time.”<br /><br />"We praise the banker that we may overdraw our account, and find good qualities in the highwayman in the hope that he may spare our pockets. I mean everything that I have said. I have the greatest contempt for optimism. As for a spoiled life, no life is spoiled but one whose growth is arrested. If you want to mar a nature, you have merely to reform it."</em> </blockquote>Words to live by. LOL! This is surely the most quo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14971157547436964364"><blockquote>
  <em>“He was always late on principle, his principle being that punctuality is the thief of time.”<br /><br />"We praise the banker that we may overdraw our account, and find good qualities in the highwayman in the hope that he may spare our pockets. I mean everything that I have said. I have the greatest contempt for optimism. As for a spoiled life, no life is spoiled but one whose growth is arrested. If you want to mar a nature, you have merely to reform it."</em>
</blockquote>Words to live by. LOL! This is surely the most quotable book I have ever read. I only chose the above quotes for a good giggle, there are many more pithy or profound ones in this novel. Besides being the most quotable book it is also one of the most misrepresented by pop culture. The movie adaptations tend to focus on the horror aspect of the book as if Wilde was a precursor to Lovecraft or something. <em>The Picture of Dorian Gray</em> is a more cerebral and allegorical than Hollywood would have you believe.<br /><br />As with most classics I picked an audiobook version and where possible I opt for the free <a rel="nofollow" href="https://librivox.org/">Librivox</a> version over the commercial Audible one. I only require that the books are reasonably well read; this happens to be one of the good ones which I can recommend with a couple of minor reservations (more on that later). What I did not realize though is that Oscar Wilde wrote two editions of this book. The original was first published in 1890, and the considerably longer (and less overtly “gay”) 1891 edition followed in response to less than enthusiastic critics’ reviews. Any way, this Librivox version is of the original edition consisting of a mere 13 chapters instead of 20.<br /><br />From the first few pages I was bowled over by the barrage of witticisms from Lord Henry Wotton who seems to have outrageous views on just about everything, and he can talk the hind legs off a donkey. Every “willful paradox” that comes out of his mouth is a gem. The first chapter alone is worth the price of the book (it’s free in Guttenberg e-book format any way). Oscar Wilde is famous for his wit and this book provides ample evidence, he did not so much write as <em>orchestrated</em> the language to create a work of art. The initial hilarity at the beginning of the book soon gives way to a much darker story and eventually culminates in a horrifying climax.<br /><br />The central characters, like everything else in this book, are very well written. The artist Basil Hallward is decent, honest and kind (not to mention probably gay), the eponymous Dorian starts off as a naïve young gentleman and fairly quickly morphs into an infamous cad. As for the amazing Lord Henry, unfortunately for Dorian he is the sort of man who likes to talk people into committing all kinds of debauchery but never does it himself, as poor Basil points out early in the book.<br /><br />I first read this book many years ago I remember liking the first few chapters very well but somehow when I first signed up to Goodreads I rated it at 3 stars as I was adding books to my bookshelf for the first time. For life of me I could not remember what the problem was. Well, I do now that I have just reread it. In spite of being extremely witty and hilarious at times this is not an entirely easy read; not because of the descent in tone into grimness, I don’t mind that at all. As it turned out the issue is only one chapter. If not for this very odd chapter the novel is actually quite easy to read.<br /><br />I am talking about the lengthy Chapter 9 (1890 edition) which is Chapter 11 in the second edition (1891). This chapter takes place after Dorian has decided to adopt a hedonistic life style and reinvents himself as a very bad boy (but oh so elegant and well coiffed) under the wicked influence of Lord Henry. Almost the entire chapter is tangential to the story and consists of Wilde’s rumination on jewelry, embroidery, art and beauty etc. I dozed off a bit during this chapter (50 minutes narration, I am not sure what the page count is, 30 at least). I think Wilde should have placed it as an appendix, in fact after finishing the book I went back to read this particular chapter just to make sure I didn’t miss anything. There is a little plot in there somewhere but you have to stay awake the entire time not to miss it.<br /><br />This audiobook version I just reviewed is read very nicely by John Gonzalez. My only reservations are that the book is set in England and all the characters are English while Mr. Gonzalez is an American, still, better a book well read in American accent than badly read by an Englishman. My other reservation is that there is a little bit of hiss in the background.<br /><br />In any case this is a fantastic book and I will have to read the second edition before too long.<br /><br />________________________<br />Notes: Free audiobook editions:<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://librivox.org/the-picture-of-dorian-gray-by-oscar-wilde/">Link to the 1890 edition</a> read by John Gonzalez.<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://librivox.org/the-picture-of-dorian-gray-1891-version-by-oscar-wilde/">Link to the 1891 edition</a> read by Bob Neufeld.<br /><br />For a hilariously unconventional review I recommend taking a gander at this <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s8iAuBdQJUg">Thug Notes review</a> on Youtube.<br /><em>"My man Wilde had to rewrite the book coz them publishers weren't chillin' on the bro on bro action!".</em><br />(Paraphrased from memory)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14971157547436964364">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_180983766">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180983766" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="left imgcol" title="K.D. Absolutely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449978413p2/2156200.jpg" alt="K.D. Absolutely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/180983766?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 03, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="user" title="K.D. Absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/122634.The_Picture_of_Dorian_Gray" title="Paperback 9780140620337" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010); Filipinos Group Read for July 2011
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core" class="actionLinkLite">1001-core</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=501" class="actionLinkLite">501</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer180983766">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14142114546245251529">My third time to read an Oscar Wilde’s work and I still like it. However, I prefer the first two: <em>De Profundis (Out of the Dephts)</em> and <em>The Happy Prince and Other Stories</em>. I liked his poignant and brilliant lamentations in the first and his adept and crisp storytelling in the 12 short stories in the second. Those two reasons, in my opinion, are not here in his only novel, <em>The Picture of Dorian Gray</em>.<br /><br />This tells the story of a strikingly handsome young man, <strong>Dorian Gray</strong> that is badly influenced by ar</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14142114546245251529">My third time to read an Oscar Wilde’s work and I still like it. However, I prefer the first two: <em>De Profundis (Out of the Dephts)</em> and <em>The Happy Prince and Other Stories</em>. I liked his poignant and brilliant lamentations in the first and his adept and crisp storytelling in the 12 short stories in the second. Those two reasons, in my opinion, are not here in his only novel, <em>The Picture of Dorian Gray</em>.<br /><br />This tells the story of a strikingly handsome young man, <strong>Dorian Gray</strong> that is badly influenced by aristocratic art enthusiast <strong>Lord Henry Wotton</strong> into believing that Dorian’s beauty is the only thing that matters with him. Disturbed by the thought of losing his beauty due to old age, he wishes that the painting drawn by <strong>Basil Hallward</strong> be the one to get old so that he will be able to preserve his beauty. Due to unknown reason (as there is no mention in the book about his deal with the devil) this becomes the reality and he remains 19 years old when he is supposed to be 37. Thinking that he is destined to immortality, he becomes addicted to vices and he committed crimes.<br /><br />What I liked about the book were its brilliant dialogues and biting satire. Lord Henry has this propensity in saying his deep philosophical musings on everything and it amused me even if I do not agree with some of his views or rants. The depiction of Victorian England is also interesting and can be compared to the Austen’s settings. I always find other cultures of foregone centuries interesting especially if it is vividly described in a classic literature written by one of the masters. I also liked that there is a moral lesson in the end: <strong>
  <em>crime does not pay.</em>
</strong><br /><br />What I did not like are the following:<br /><br />(1) The references to male beauty – very Edward Cullen. I have my own charm and I know if a man is handsome or plain-looking but I cannot relate to people watching male beauty pageant. This is just not my cup of tea.<br /><br />(2) My wrong expectation – thought this was scary. I remember reading a <em>komiks</em> adaptation of this story when I was still a young boy in the province and, even at that age, it did not scare me. The picture getting all the wrinkles and crow’s feet while the young man beauty remains unblemished and perfect is so implausible that I did not give another minute to think about it. Now, when the Filipinos group here in Goodreads chose this to be our July’s Classic Book, I thought that reading the story from its original source and told by Wilde would give me complete scare. I thought I expected too much from him. The devil did not even have a cameo role. Mind you, a less popular gothic novel, Walpole’s <em>The Castle of Otranto</em> was even able to give me a slight jitters. I did not have any here.<br /><br />(3) There is not a single character who I can emphasize with. Wiki mentioned that in an interview with Wilde, the author mentioned that he was part of all the 3 main characters: Dorian, Basil and Lord Henry. That part did not mention which characteristics of each would be that part of him as a person. Based on his internet pictures, Wilde did not look like Dorian so I guess Dorian was what he fantasized for a male lover (like Alfred Douglas?). Oh yes, I would imagine that Wilde’s wit and gift of gab should be comparable to Lord Henry’s. And Basil who was afraid to show Dorian’s portrait? That must be his closeted self.<br /><br />Although it is far from being an amazing book, overall, I still liked it and absolutely did not regret reading it. After all, this is a classic.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14142114546245251529">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_675901670">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/675901670" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl" class="left imgcol" title="Cheryl"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442870534p2/11255849.jpg" alt="Cheryl" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/675901670?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl" class="user" title="Cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1857397.The_Picture_of_Dorian_Gray" title="Paperback 9781593080259" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=classic-lit" class="actionLinkLite">classic-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=the-psyche" class="actionLinkLite">the-psyche</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer675901670">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14985540554169310897"><em> <strong> <blockquote>“What sort of life would his be if, day and night, shadows of his crime were to peer at him from silent corners, to mock him from secret places, to whisper in his ear as he sat at the feast, to wake him with icy fingers as he lay asleep!”</blockquote> </strong> </em><br />Everyone Dorian Gray came into contact with, was disbarred from regular society, his or her life tarnished, his or her future vanished. Was Dorian Gray just one person, or the embodiment of something else? <br /><br /><em> <blockquote>To cure the soul by means of the senses, and the se</blockquote></em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14985540554169310897"><em>
  <strong>
    <blockquote>“What sort of life would his be if, day and night, shadows of his crime were to peer at him from silent corners, to mock him from secret places, to whisper in his ear as he sat at the feast, to wake him with icy fingers as he lay asleep!”</blockquote>
  </strong>
</em><br />Everyone Dorian Gray came into contact with, was disbarred from regular society, his or her life tarnished, his or her future vanished. Was Dorian Gray just one person, or the embodiment of something else? <br /><br /><em>
  <blockquote>To cure the soul by means of the senses, and the senses by means of the soul.</blockquote>
</em><br />Those words rang in his ears and in mine, as I pondered their deeper context. Sometimes books leave memorable lines that you remember, and this is one. <br /><br />The picture of Dorian Gray is a larger-than-life portrait created by the artist, Basil Hallward, as an ode to the man Basil worships. Yet the moment he creates it, Basil senses his wrong. It is idolatry, he tells Dorian later, “you became to me the visible incarnation of the unseen ideal whose memory haunts us artists like an exquisite dream.” Ok seriously, this is when I’m wondering, but why? What is it about this character that has everyone so attracted to him, and I, the reader, can’t seem to get close enough to him in order to like him? I went through my checklist, does he exhibit: Charisma? No. Vulnerability? No. Intellectual stability? No. Sincerity? No. What then? <br /><br /><em>
  <strong>
    <blockquote>The picture, changed or unchanged, would be to him the visible emblem of conscience.</blockquote>
  </strong>
</em><br />This is one of those books whose first scenes you have to read carefully, because it sets the thematic stage for everything that will transpire later. Just when you sense the unveiling of a love story, you get the unearthing of philosophical meanderings. Is this a gothic novel about a tortured protagonist, or the celebration of art through the magical realism of a painting? Or is this simply a manifesto of conscience and passion, two themes that seem emboldened in almost every chapter?<br /><br />When he met Dorian Gray, the character Basil said, <em>
  <strong>“something seemed to tell me that I was on the verge of a terrible crisis in my life. I had a strange feeling fate had in store for me exquisite joys and exquisite sorrows.”</strong>
</em>This gave me pause to think, didn’t Wilde’s life also take a downturn turn once he met Douglas? Although, according to scholars, it is suspected that Dorian Gray was actually modeled after an artist named John Gray, a friend of Wilde. <br /><br />I hate that Oscar Wilde had to suffer for his art because of his lifestyle; for one to stand trial for one’s sexuality—how heartrending (although I will save my commentary until I read a biography or two because there is vague mention of his "gift-giving" to students and so forth…) When this book came out, there were concerns about homosexuality, so according to Wilde’s scholars, most of the scenes were edited. There are no blatant sexual scenes here, but what if there were--so what? Is this not why we have a choice between different genres, like erotica for instance? While there are a few subtleties between Gray, Basil, and Lord Henry, the most indicative scene is the one with Adrian Singleton, the man whose life has been ruined because of his relationship with Dorian Gray. In the end, Adrian spends his time in an opium bar, in a discarded section of town, where Dorian Gray runs into him and is immediately distraught when he sees Adrian, “memory, like a horrible malady, was eating his soul away…he wanted to be where no one would know who he was. He wanted to escape himself.” <br /><br />Perhaps loving a character takes a back seat to realizing the thematic undertones of this novel, for theme surpasses story here, <br /><blockquote>
  <em>But it appeared to Dorian Gray that the true nature of the senses had never been understood, and that they had remained savage and animal merely because the world had sought to starve them into submission or to kill them by pain, instead of aiming at making them elements of a new spirituality, of which a fine instinct for beauty was to be the dominant characteristic.</em>
</blockquote><br />Still, I wished that Lord Henry Wotton, Hallward’s friend and Dorian Gray’s companion and mentor, would just stop talking. Just as Lord Henry thinks he knows the world, he is so sure that he also knows women. For women are either worshipping men, (<em>“women treat us just as humanity treats its gods. They worship us, and are always bothering us to do something for them”</em>), or a man was to be <em>“happy with any woman as long as he does not love her,”</em> and so on. Lord Henry continued to fill the novel with blocks of dialogue that could have easily been expository narrative. The characters seemed to be sit-ins for themes, and even as I followed Dorian through the dungeon of his despair and was with him as he committed his violent crime, I felt as if I never did know him. Who was Dorian Gray, really? Even he didn’t know. Perhaps the answer really is in the painting. <br /><br />Sorry, Oscar, but I do believe James Joyce showcased a better portrait. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14985540554169310897">...more</a>

          </span>

Denounced as vulgar by the literary establishment, Three Men in a Boat nevertheless caught the spirit of the times. The expansion of education and the increase in office workers created a new mass readership, and Jerome\'s book was especially popular among the \'clerking classes\' who longed to be \'free from that fretful haste, that vehement striving, that is every day becoming more and more the bane of nineteenth-century life.\'So popular did it prove that Jerome reunited his heroes for a bicycle tour of Germany. Despite some sharp, and with hindsight, prophetic observations of the country, Three Men on the Bummel describes an equally picaresque journey constrained only \'by the necessity of getting back within a given time to the point from which one started\'.For more than seventy years, Penguin has been the leading publisher of classic literature in the English-speaking world. Gulliver’s Travels is, of course, his world renowned masterpiece in th&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText1135158138237177796\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;The originality, concentrated power and ‘fierce indignation’ of his satirical writing have earned Jonathan Swift a reputation as the greatest prose satirist in English literature. Gulliver’s Travels is, of course, his world renowned masterpiece in the genre; however, Swift wrote other, shorter works that also offer excellent evidence of his inspired lampoonery. Perhaps the most famous of these is A Modest Proposal, in which he straight-facedly suggests that Ireland could solve its hunger problems by using its children for food. Also included in this collection are The Battle of Books, A Meditation upon a Broomstick, A Discourse Concerning the Mechanical Operations of the Spirit and An Argument Against Abolishing Christianity The story concerns Thomas Sutpen, a poor man who finds wealth and then marries into a respectable family. His ambition and extreme need for control bring about his ruin and the ruin of his family.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3565.Oscar_Wilde">About Oscar Wilde</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/3565.Oscar_Wilde"><img alt="3565" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1357460488p2/3565.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer9196498581549932619">Oscar Fingal O'Flahertie Wills Wilde was an Irish playwright, poet and author of numerous short stories and one novel. Known for his biting wit, and a plentitude of aphorisms, he became one of the most successful playwrights of the late Victorian era in London, and one of the greatest celebrities of his day. Several of his plays continue to be widely performed, especially <em>The Importance of Being E</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9196498581549932619">Oscar Fingal O'Flahertie Wills Wilde was an Irish playwright, poet and author of numerous short stories and one novel. Known for his biting wit, and a plentitude of aphorisms, he became one of the most successful playwrights of the late Victorian era in London, and one of the greatest celebrities of his day. Several of his plays continue to be widely performed, especially <em>The Importance of Being Earnest</em>. <br /><br />As the result of a widely covered series of trials, Wilde suffered a dramatic downfall and was imprisoned for two years hard labour after being convicted of "gross indecency" with other men. After Wilde was released from prison he set sail for Dieppe by the night ferry. He never returned to Ireland or Britain, and died in poverty.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9196498581549932619">...more</a>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/3565.Oscar_Wilde" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Oscar Wilde...</a>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
